<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Fish</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For fish as eaten by humans, see <a href="/wiki/Fish_as_food" title="Fish as food">Fish as food</a>. For the superclass containing the vast majority of living fish, see <a href="/wiki/Osteichthyes" title="Osteichthyes">Osteichthyes</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Fish_(disambiguation)" title="Fish (disambiguation)">Fish (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Aquatic vertebrates, usually with gills</div>
<p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r876297439">.mw-parser-output table.biota-infobox{text-align:center;width:200px;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output table.biota-infobox th.section-header{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output table.biota-infobox td.section-content{text-align:left;padding:0 0.25em}.mw-parser-output table.biota-infobox td.list-section{text-align:left;padding:0 0.25em}.mw-parser-output table.biota-infobox td.taxon-section{text-align:center;padding:0 0.25em}.mw-parser-output table.biota-infobox td.image-section{text-align:center;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output table.biota-infobox table.taxonomy{margin:0 auto;text-align:left;background:transparent;padding:2px}.mw-parser-output table.biota-infobox table.taxonomy tr{vertical-align:top}.mw-parser-output table.biota-infobox table.taxonomy td{padding:1px}</style>
</p>

<td class="section-content" colspan="2">
<dl><dd><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrapoda" title="Tetrapoda">Tetrapods</a></dd>
<dd><abbr aria-label="Extinct" style="border: none; text-decoration: none; cursor: inherit; font-weight: normal; font-style: normal;" title="Extinct">†</abbr><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conodonts" title="Conodonts">Conodonts</a></dd></dl>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Fish</b> are <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_animal" title="Aquatic animal">aquatic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Craniate" title="Craniate">craniate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gill</a>-bearing animals that lack <a href="/wiki/Limb_(anatomy)" title="Limb (anatomy)">limbs</a> with <a href="/wiki/Digit_(anatomy)" title="Digit (anatomy)">digits</a>. Included in this definition are the living <a href="/wiki/Hagfish" title="Hagfish">hagfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lampreys</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes">cartilaginous</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bony_fish" title="Bony fish">bony fish</a> as well as various extinct related groups. Approximately 95% of living fish species are ray-finned fish, belonging to the class <a href="/wiki/Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii">Actinopterygii</a>, with around 99% of those being <a href="/wiki/Teleost" title="Teleost">teleosts</a>.
</p><p>The earliest organisms that can be classified as fish were soft-bodied <a href="/wiki/Chordate" title="Chordate">chordates</a> that first appeared during the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a> period. Although they lacked a <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">true spine</a>, they possessed <a href="/wiki/Notochord" title="Notochord">notochords</a> which allowed them to be more agile than their invertebrate counterparts. Fish would continue to evolve through the <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a> era, diversifying into a wide variety of forms. Many fish of the Paleozoic developed <a href="/wiki/Placodermi" title="Placodermi">external armor</a> that protected them from predators. The first fish with <a href="/wiki/Jaw" title="Jaw">jaws</a> appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Silurian" title="Silurian">Silurian</a> period, after which many (such as <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a>) became formidable marine predators rather than just the prey of <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>.
</p><p>Most fish are <a href="/wiki/Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm">ectothermic</a> ("cold-blooded"), allowing their body temperatures to vary as ambient temperatures change, though some of the large active swimmers like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_shark" title="White shark">white shark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a> can hold a higher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_temperature" title="Core temperature">core temperature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Fish can acoustically communicate with each other, most often in the context of feeding, aggression or courtship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bioacoustics_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bioacoustics-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fish are abundant in most bodies of water. They can be found in nearly all aquatic environments, from high mountain streams (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Salvelinus" title="Salvelinus">char</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gudgeon_(fish)" title="Gudgeon (fish)">gudgeon</a>) to the <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_zone" title="Abyssal zone">abyssal</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Hadal_zone" title="Hadal zone">hadal</a> depths of the deepest oceans (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cusk-eels" title="Cusk-eels">cusk-eels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Snailfish" title="Snailfish">snailfish</a>), although no species has yet been documented in the deepest 25% of the ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> With 34,300 described species, fish exhibit greater species diversity than any other group of vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fish are an important resource for humans worldwide, especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_(food)" title="Fish (food)">as food</a>. Commercial and subsistence fishers hunt fish in <a href="/wiki/Wild_fisheries" title="Wild fisheries">wild fisheries</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_farm" title="Fish farm">farm</a> them in ponds or in cages in the ocean (in <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a>). They are also caught by <a href="/wiki/Recreational_fishing" title="Recreational fishing">recreational fishers</a>, kept as pets, raised by <a href="/wiki/Fishkeeping" title="Fishkeeping">fishkeepers</a>, and exhibited in public <a href="/wiki/Aquarium" title="Aquarium">aquaria</a>. Fish have had a role in culture through the ages, serving as deities, religious symbols, and as the subjects of art, books and movies.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">Tetrapods</a> (<a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>) emerged within <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">lobe-finned fishes</a>, so <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cladistically" title="Cladistically">cladistically</a> they are fish as well. However, traditionally fish (<b>pisces</b> or <b>ichthyes</b>) are rendered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a> by excluding the tetrapods, and are therefore not considered a formal taxonomic grouping in <a href="/wiki/Systematics" title="Systematics">systematic biology</a>, unless it is used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cladistic" title="Cladistic">cladistic</a> sense, including tetrapods,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> although usually "<a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a>" is preferred and used for this purpose (fish plus tetrapods) instead. Furthermore, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetaceans" title="Cetaceans">cetaceans</a>, although mammals, have often been considered fish by various cultures and time periods.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word for <i>fish</i> in English and the other <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Fisch</i></span>; <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a> <span title="Gothic-language text"><i lang="got">fisks</i></span>) is inherited from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a>, and is related to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">piscis</i></span> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Irish_language" title="Old Irish language">Old Irish</a> <span title="Old Irish (to 900)-language text"><i lang="sga">īasc</i></span>, though the exact root is unknown; some authorities reconstruct an <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> root <span title="Proto-Indo-European-language text">*<i lang="ine">peysk-</i></span>, attested only in <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">Italic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The English word once had a much broader usage than its current biological meaning. Names such as <a href="/wiki/Starfish" title="Starfish">starfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a> attest to almost any fully aquatic animal (including whales) once being <i>fish</i>. "Correcting" such names (e.g. to <i>sea star</i>) is an attempt to retroactively apply the current meaning of <i>fish</i> to words that were coined when it had a different meaning.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_fish" title="Evolution of fish">Evolution of fish</a></div><p>Fish, as vertebrata, developed as sister of the tunicata. As the tetrapods emerged deep within the fishes group, as sister of the lungfish, characteristics of fish are typically shared by tetrapods, including having vertebrae and a cranium.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:D_Terrelli.png"><img alt="Drawing of animal with large mouth, long tail, very small dorsal fins, and pectoral fins that attach towards the bottom of the body, resembling lizard legs in scale and development.[12]" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1160" data-file-width="2433" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/D_Terrelli.png/220px-D_Terrelli.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/D_Terrelli.png/330px-D_Terrelli.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/D_Terrelli.png/440px-D_Terrelli.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:D_Terrelli.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Dunkleosteus" title="Dunkleosteus">Dunkleosteus</a></i> was a gigantic, 10-metre (33 ft) long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_fish" title="Prehistoric fish">prehistoric fish</a> of class Placodermi.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3203" data-file-width="5271" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg/220px-Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg/330px-Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg/440px-Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lower jaw of the placoderm <i><a href="/wiki/Eastmanosteus" title="Eastmanosteus">Eastmanosteus</a> pustulosus</i>, showing the shearing structures ("teeth") on its oral surface; from the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a></div></div></div>
<p>Early fish from the fossil record are represented by a group of small, jawless, armored fish known as <a href="/wiki/Ostracoderm" title="Ostracoderm">ostracoderms</a>. Jawless fish lineages are mostly extinct. An extant clade, the <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lampreys</a> may approximate ancient pre-jawed fish. The first jaws are found in <a href="/wiki/Placodermi" title="Placodermi">Placodermi</a> fossils. They lacked distinct teeth, having instead the oral surfaces of their jaw plates modified to serve the various purposes of teeth. The diversity of jawed vertebrates may indicate the evolutionary advantage of a <a href="/wiki/Fish_jaw" title="Fish jaw">jawed mouth</a>. It is unclear if the advantage of a hinged jaw is greater biting force, improved respiration, or a combination of factors.
</p><p>Fish may have evolved from a creature similar to a coral-like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_squirt" title="Sea squirt">sea squirt</a>, whose larvae resemble primitive fish in important ways. The first ancestors of fish may have <a href="/wiki/Neoteny" title="Neoteny">kept the larval form into adulthood</a> (as some sea squirts do today).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</span></h3>
<p>Fishes are a <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">paraphyletic</a> group: that is, any <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> containing all fish also contains the <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>, which are not fish (though they include fish-shaped forms, such as <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" title="Cetacea">Whales and Dolphins</a> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct" title="Extinct">extinct</a> <a href="/wiki/Ichthyopterygia" title="Ichthyopterygia">ichthyosaurs</a>, which acquired a fish-like body shape due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_aquatic_adaptation" title="Secondary aquatic adaptation">secondary aquatic adaptation</a>, see <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_cetaceans" title="Evolution of cetaceans">evolution of cetaceans</a>). 
</p><p>The following <a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">cladogram</a> shows <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a> - some with, some without <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxa" title="Extant taxa">extant</a> relatives - that are traditionally considered as "fishes" (<a href="/wiki/Cyan" title="Cyan">cyan</a> line) and the tetrapods (four-limbed vertebrates), which are mostly terrestrial. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct" title="Extinct">Extinct</a> groups are marked with a <a href="/wiki/Dagger_(mark)" title="Dagger (mark)">dagger</a> (†).
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r984819063">.mw-parser-output table.clade{border-spacing:0;margin:0;font-size:100%;line-height:100%;border-collapse:separate;width:auto}.mw-parser-output table.clade table.clade{width:100%;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-label{width:0.7em;padding:0 0.15em;vertical-align:bottom;text-align:center;border-left:1px solid;border-bottom:1px solid;white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-fixed-width{overflow:hidden;text-overflow:ellipsis}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-fixed-width:hover{overflow:visible}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-label.first{border-left:none;border-right:none}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-label.reverse{border-left:none;border-right:1px solid}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel{padding:0 0.15em;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;border-left:1px solid;white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel:hover{overflow:visible}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel.last{border-left:none;border-right:none}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel.reverse{border-left:none;border-right:1px solid}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-bar{vertical-align:middle;text-align:left;padding:0 0.5em;position:relative}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-bar.reverse{text-align:right;position:relative}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leaf{border:0;padding:0;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leafR{border:0;padding:0;text-align:right}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leaf.reverse{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output table.clade:hover span.linkA{background-color:yellow}.mw-parser-output table.clade:hover span.linkB{background-color:green}</style>

<td class="clade-slabel last"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Craniata" title="Craniata">Craniata</a>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1441" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg/220px-Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg/330px-Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg/440px-Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Leedsichthys" title="Leedsichthys">Leedsichthys</a>,</i> of the subclass Actinopterygii, is the largest known fish, with estimates in 2005 putting its maximum size at 16 metres (52 ft).</div></div></div>
<p>Fishes are a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a> group and for this reason, groups such as the class <i>Pisces</i> seen in older reference works are no longer used in formal classifications. Traditional classification divides fish into three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">classes</a>, and with extinct forms sometimes classified within the tree, sometimes as their own classes:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romer,_A_1977_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Romer,_A_1977-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Class <a href="/wiki/Agnatha" title="Agnatha">Agnatha</a> (jawless fish)
<ul><li>Subclass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclostomata" title="Cyclostomata">Cyclostomata</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hagfish" title="Hagfish">hagfish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lampreys</a>)</li>
<li>Subclass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ostracodermi" title="Ostracodermi">Ostracodermi</a> (armoured jawless fish) †</li></ul></li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes">Chondrichthyes</a> (cartilaginous fish)
<ul><li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Elasmobranchii" title="Elasmobranchii">Elasmobranchii</a> (<a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Batoidea" title="Batoidea">rays</a>)</li>
<li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Holocephali" title="Holocephali">Holocephali</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chimaera" title="Chimaera">chimaeras</a> and extinct relatives)</li></ul></li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Placodermi" title="Placodermi">Placodermi</a> (armoured fish) †</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Acanthodii" title="Acanthodii">Acanthodii</a> ("spiny sharks", sometimes classified under bony fishes)†</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Osteichthyes" title="Osteichthyes">Osteichthyes</a> (bony fish)
<ul><li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii">Actinopterygii</a> (ray finned fishes)</li>
<li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">Sarcopterygii</a> (fleshy finned fishes, ancestors of tetrapods)</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>The above scheme is the one most commonly encountered in non-specialist and general works. Many of the above groups are paraphyletic, in that they have given rise to successive groups: Agnathans are ancestral to Chondrichthyes, who again have given rise to Acanthodiians, the ancestors of Osteichthyes. With the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_nomenclature" title="Phylogenetic nomenclature">phylogenetic nomenclature</a>, the fishes has been split up into a more detailed scheme, with the following major groups:
</p>
<ul><li>Class Myxini (<a href="/wiki/Hagfish" title="Hagfish">hagfish</a>)</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Pteraspidomorphi" title="Pteraspidomorphi">Pteraspidomorphi</a> † (early jawless fish)</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Thelodonti" title="Thelodonti">Thelodonti</a> †</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Anaspida" title="Anaspida">Anaspida</a> †</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Hyperoartia" title="Hyperoartia">Petromyzontida or Hyperoartia</a>
<ul><li>Petromyzontidae (<a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lampreys</a>)</li></ul></li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Conodont" title="Conodont">Conodonta</a> (conodonts) †</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Cephalaspidomorphi" title="Cephalaspidomorphi">Cephalaspidomorphi</a> † (early jawless fish)
<ul><li>(unranked) <a href="/wiki/Galeaspida" title="Galeaspida">Galeaspida</a> †</li>
<li>(unranked) <a href="/wiki/Pituriaspida" title="Pituriaspida">Pituriaspida</a> †</li>
<li>(unranked) <a href="/wiki/Osteostraci" title="Osteostraci">Osteostraci</a> †</li></ul></li>
<li>Infraphylum <a href="/wiki/Gnathostomata" title="Gnathostomata">Gnathostomata</a> (jawed vertebrates)
<ul><li>Class <a href="/wiki/Placodermi" title="Placodermi">Placodermi</a> † (armoured fish)</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes">Chondrichthyes</a> (cartilaginous fish)</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Acanthodii" title="Acanthodii">Acanthodii</a> † (spiny sharks)</li>
<li>Superclass <a href="/wiki/Osteichthyes" title="Osteichthyes">Osteichthyes</a> (bony fish)
<ul><li>Class <a href="/wiki/Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii">Actinopterygii</a> (ray-finned fish)
<ul><li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Chondrostei" title="Chondrostei">Chondrostei</a>
<ul><li>Order <a href="/wiki/Acipenseriformes" title="Acipenseriformes">Acipenseriformes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sturgeon" title="Sturgeon">sturgeons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paddlefish" title="Paddlefish">paddlefishes</a>)</li>
<li>Order <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polypteriformes" title="Polypteriformes">Polypteriformes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Reedfish" title="Reedfish">reedfishes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bichir" title="Bichir">bichirs</a>).</li></ul></li>
<li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Neopterygii" title="Neopterygii">Neopterygii</a>
<ul><li>Infraclass <a href="/wiki/Holostei" title="Holostei">Holostei</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gar" title="Gar">gars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bowfin" title="Bowfin">bowfins</a>)</li>
<li>Infraclass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teleostei" title="Teleostei">Teleostei</a> (many orders of common fish)</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">Sarcopterygii</a> (lobe-finned fish)
<ul><li>Subclass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actinistia" title="Actinistia">Actinistia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Coelacanth" title="Coelacanth">coelacanths</a>)</li>
<li>Subclass Dipnoi (<a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">lungfish</a>, sister group to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrapods" title="Tetrapods">tetrapods</a>)</li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>† – indicates extinct taxon<br/>Some palaeontologists contend that because <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conodonta" title="Conodonta">Conodonta</a> are <a href="/wiki/Chordate" title="Chordate">chordates</a>, they are primitive fish. For a fuller treatment of this taxonomy, see the <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> article.
</p><p>The position of <a href="/wiki/Hagfish" title="Hagfish">hagfish</a> in the phylum Chordata is not settled. Phylogenetic research in 1998 and 1999 supported the idea that the hagfish and the lampreys form a natural group, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclostomata" title="Cyclostomata">Cyclostomata</a>, that is a sister group of the Gnathostomata.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The various fish groups account for more than half of vertebrate species. As of 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson20064–5_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson20064–5-19">[19]</a></sup> there are almost 28,000 known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> species, of which almost 27,000 are bony fish, with 970 <a href="/wiki/Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes">sharks, rays, and chimeras</a> and about 108 hagfish and lampreys. A third of these species fall within the nine largest families; from largest to smallest, these families are <a href="/wiki/Cyprinidae" title="Cyprinidae">Cyprinidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gobiidae" title="Gobiidae">Gobiidae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cichlidae" title="Cichlidae">Cichlidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Characidae" title="Characidae">Characidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Loricariidae" title="Loricariidae">Loricariidae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balitoridae" title="Balitoridae">Balitoridae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serranidae" title="Serranidae">Serranidae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labridae" title="Labridae">Labridae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scorpaenidae" title="Scorpaenidae">Scorpaenidae</a>. About 64 families are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotypic" title="Monotypic">monotypic</a>, containing only one species. The final total of extant species may grow to exceed 32,500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson20063_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson20063-20">[20]</a></sup> Each year, new <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> are discovered and <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy" title="Taxonomy">scientifically described</a>. As of 2016,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-21">[21]</a></sup> there are over 32,000 documented species of bony fish and over 1,100 species of cartilaginous fish. Species are lost through <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biodiversity_crisis" title="Biodiversity crisis">biodiversity crisis</a>). Recent examples are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psephurus" title="Psephurus">Chinese paddlefish</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Smooth_handfish" title="Smooth handfish">smooth handfish</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity">Diversity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diversity_of_fish" title="Diversity of fish">Diversity of fish</a></div>

</ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leafydragon.jpg"><img alt="Photo of fish with many narrow, straight appendages. Some are end in points, and others are longer, ending in two or three approximately flat, triangular flaps, each with a dark spot." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="572" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Leafydragon.jpg/220px-Leafydragon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Leafydragon.jpg/330px-Leafydragon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Leafydragon.jpg/440px-Leafydragon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leafydragon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A relative of the seahorses, the <a href="/wiki/Leafy_seadragon" title="Leafy seadragon">leafy seadragon</a>'s appendages allow it to camouflage (in the form of <a href="/wiki/Crypsis" title="Crypsis">crypsis</a>) with the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="729" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png/220px-Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png/330px-Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png/440px-Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The psychedelic <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mandarin_dragonet" title="Mandarin dragonet">mandarin dragonet</a></i> is one of only two animal species known to have blue colouring because of cellular pigment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The term "fish" most precisely describes any non-<a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapod</a> <a href="/wiki/Craniate" title="Craniate">craniate</a> (i.e. an animal with a skull and in most cases a backbone) that has <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a> throughout life and whose limbs, if any, are in the shape of fins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson20062_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson20062-23">[23]</a></sup> Unlike groupings such as birds or <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, fish are not a single <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> but a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a> collection of <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Hagfish" title="Hagfish">hagfishes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lampreys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes">sharks and rays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii">ray-finned fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coelacanth" title="Coelacanth">coelacanths</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">lungfish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey19973_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey19973-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Indeed, lungfish and coelacanths are closer relatives of <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a> (such as mammals, birds, <a href="/wiki/Lissamphibia" title="Lissamphibia">amphibians</a>, etc.) than of other fish such as ray-finned fish or sharks, so the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_common_ancestor" title="Last common ancestor">last common ancestor</a> of all fish is also an ancestor to tetrapods. As paraphyletic groups are no longer recognised in modern <a href="/wiki/Systematics" title="Systematics">systematic biology</a>, the use of the term "fish" as a biological group must be avoided.
</p><p>Many types of <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_animal" title="Aquatic animal">aquatic animals</a> commonly referred to as "fish" are not fish in the sense given above; examples include <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Starfish" title="Starfish">starfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crayfish" title="Crayfish">crayfish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>. In earlier times, even biologists did not make a distinction – sixteenth century natural historians classified also <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a>, whales, <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a>, even <a href="/wiki/Hippopotamus" title="Hippopotamus">hippopotamuses</a>, as well as a host of aquatic invertebrates, as fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-integrated_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-integrated-26">[26]</a></sup> However, according to the definition above, all mammals, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetacean" title="Cetacean">cetaceans</a> like whales and dolphins, are not fish. In some contexts, especially in <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a>, the true fish are referred to as <b>finfish</b> (or <b>fin fish</b>) to distinguish them from these other animals.
</p><p>A typical fish is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ectothermic" title="Ectothermic">ectothermic</a>, has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Streamlines,_streaklines_and_pathlines" title="Streamlines, streaklines and pathlines">streamlined</a> body for rapid swimming, extracts oxygen from water using gills or uses an accessory breathing organ to breathe atmospheric oxygen, has two sets of paired fins, usually one or two (rarely three) dorsal fins, an anal fin, and a tail fin, has jaws, has skin that is usually covered with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scale_(zoology)" title="Scale (zoology)">scales</a>, and lays eggs.
</p><p>Each criterion has exceptions. <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">Tuna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swordfish" title="Swordfish">swordfish</a>, and some species of <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a> show <a href="/wiki/Warm-blooded#Temperature_control_in_cold-blooded_animals" title="Warm-blooded">some warm-blooded adaptations</a> – they can heat their bodies significantly above ambient water temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey19973_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey19973-24">[24]</a></sup> Streamlining and swimming performance varies from fish such as tuna, <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carangidae" title="Carangidae">jacks</a> that can cover 10–20 body-lengths per second to species such as <a href="/wiki/Eel" title="Eel">eels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Batoidea" title="Batoidea">rays</a> that swim no more than 0.5 body-lengths per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997103_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997103-27">[27]</a></sup> Many groups of freshwater fish extract oxygen from the air as well as from the water using a variety of different structures. <a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">Lungfish</a> have paired lungs similar to those of tetrapods, <a href="/wiki/Gourami" title="Gourami">gouramis</a> have a structure called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labyrinth_organ" title="Labyrinth organ">labyrinth organ</a> that performs a similar function, while many catfish, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Corydoras" title="Corydoras">Corydoras</a></i> extract oxygen via the intestine or stomach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199753–57_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199753–57-28">[28]</a></sup> Body shape and the arrangement of the fins is highly variable, covering such seemingly un-fishlike forms as <a href="/wiki/Seahorse" title="Seahorse">seahorses</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pufferfish" title="Pufferfish">pufferfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglerfish" title="Anglerfish">anglerfish</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saccopharyngiformes" title="Saccopharyngiformes">gulpers</a>. Similarly, the surface of the skin may be naked (as in <a href="/wiki/Moray_eel" title="Moray eel">moray eels</a>), or covered with scales of a variety of different types usually defined as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placoid_scale" title="Placoid scale">placoid</a> (typical of sharks and rays), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scale_(zoology)#Cosmoid_scales" title="Scale (zoology)">cosmoid</a> (fossil lungfish and coelacanths), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganoid" title="Ganoid">ganoid</a> (various fossil fish but also living <a href="/wiki/Gar" title="Gar">gars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bichir" title="Bichir">bichirs</a>), <a href="/wiki/Fish_scale#Leptoid_scales" title="Fish scale">cycloid</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ctenoid" title="Ctenoid">ctenoid</a> (these last two are found on most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bony_fish" title="Bony fish">bony fish</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199733–36_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199733–36-29">[29]</a></sup> There are even fish that live mostly on land or lay their eggs on land near water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mudskipper" title="Mudskipper">Mudskippers</a> feed and interact with one another on mudflats and go underwater to hide in their burrows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> A single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Undescribed_species" title="Undescribed species">undescribed species</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Phreatobius" title="Phreatobius">Phreatobius</a></i> has been called a true "land fish" as this worm-like catfish strictly lives among waterlogged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leaf_litter" title="Leaf litter">leaf litter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Many species live in <a href="/wiki/Underground_lake" title="Underground lake">underground lakes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Underground_river" title="Underground river">underground rivers</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a> and are popularly known as <a href="/wiki/Cavefish" title="Cavefish">cavefish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fish range in size from the huge 16-metre (52 ft) <a href="/wiki/Whale_shark" title="Whale shark">whale shark</a> to the tiny 8-millimetre (0.3 in) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stout_infantfish" title="Stout infantfish">stout infantfish</a>.
</p><p>Fish <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> diversity is roughly divided equally between marine (oceanic) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a> ecosystems. <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">Coral reefs</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific" title="Indo-Pacific">Indo-Pacific</a> constitute the center of diversity for marine fishes, whereas continental freshwater fishes are most diverse in large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_basin" title="River basin">river basins</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforests</a>, especially the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Basin" title="Amazon Basin">Amazon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Congo_Basin" title="Congo Basin">Congo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mekong" title="Mekong">Mekong</a> basins. More than 5,600 fish species inhabit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neotropic" title="Neotropic">Neotropical</a> freshwaters alone, such that <a href="/wiki/Neotropical_fish" title="Neotropical fish">Neotropical fishes</a> represent about 10% of all <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> species on the Earth. Exceptionally rich sites in the Amazon basin, such as <a href="/wiki/Cant%C3%A3o_State_Park" title="Cantão State Park">Cantão State Park</a>, can contain more freshwater fish species than occur in all of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The deepest living fish in the ocean so far found is the Mariana snailfish (<i><a href="/wiki/Pseudoliparis_swirei" title="Pseudoliparis swirei">Pseudoliparis swirei</a></i>) which lives at deeps of 8,000 meters (26,200 feet) along the Mariana Trench near Guam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">diversity</a> of living fish (finfish) is unevenly distributed among the various groups, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teleosts" title="Teleosts">teleosts</a> making up the bulk of living fishes (96%), and over 50% of all <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-21">[21]</a></sup> The following <a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">cladogram</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Friedman_&amp;_Sallan_2012_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Friedman_&amp;_Sallan_2012-37">[37]</a></sup> shows the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">evolutionary relationships</a> of all groups of living fishes (with their respective diversity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto-21">[21]</a></sup>) and the four-limbed vertebrates (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrapods" title="Tetrapods">tetrapods</a>).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fish_evolution.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="767" data-file-width="1073" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Fish_evolution.png/220px-Fish_evolution.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Fish_evolution.png/330px-Fish_evolution.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Fish_evolution.png/440px-Fish_evolution.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fish_evolution.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">Diversity</a> of various groups of fish (and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates">vertebrates</a>) through time</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Queensland_Lungfish_(Neoceratodus_forsteri).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="705" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Queensland_Lungfish_%28Neoceratodus_forsteri%29.jpg/220px-Queensland_Lungfish_%28Neoceratodus_forsteri%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Queensland_Lungfish_%28Neoceratodus_forsteri%29.jpg/330px-Queensland_Lungfish_%28Neoceratodus_forsteri%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Queensland_Lungfish_%28Neoceratodus_forsteri%29.jpg/440px-Queensland_Lungfish_%28Neoceratodus_forsteri%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Queensland_Lungfish_(Neoceratodus_forsteri).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">Lungfish</a> are the closest living relatives of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrapods" title="Tetrapods">tetrapods</a> (four-limbed vertebrates).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amia_calva_4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Amia_calva_4.jpg/220px-Amia_calva_4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Amia_calva_4.jpg/330px-Amia_calva_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Amia_calva_4.jpg/440px-Amia_calva_4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amia_calva_4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Bowfin" title="Bowfin">bowfin</a> <i>Amia calva</i> is the sole survivor of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halecomorph" title="Halecomorph">halecomorph</a> clade.</div></div></div>
