<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy_and_physiology">Anatomy and physiology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fish_anatomy" title="Fish anatomy">Fish anatomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fish_physiology" title="Fish physiology">Fish physiology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Torskens_indre_organer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1224" data-file-width="1632" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Torskens_indre_organer.jpg/250px-Torskens_indre_organer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Torskens_indre_organer.jpg/375px-Torskens_indre_organer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Torskens_indre_organer.jpg/500px-Torskens_indre_organer.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Torskens_indre_organer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">Organs</a>: 1. <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">Liver</a>, 2. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_bladder" title="Gas bladder">Gas bladder</a>, 3. <a href="/wiki/Roe" title="Roe">Roe</a>, 4. Pyloric caeca, 5. <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">Stomach</a>, 6. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestine" title="Intestine">Intestine</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Respiration">Respiration</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_respiration" title="Aquatic respiration">Aquatic respiration</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gills">Gills</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg"><img alt="Photo of fish head split in half longitudinally with gill filaments crossing from top to bottom" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="954" data-file-width="1103" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg/220px-Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg/330px-Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg/440px-Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">Tuna</a> gills inside the head. The fish head is oriented snout-downwards, with the view looking towards the mouth.</div></div></div>
<p>Most fish exchange gases using <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a> on either side of the <a href="/wiki/Pharynx" title="Pharynx">pharynx</a>. Gills consist of threadlike structures called <a href="/wiki/Protein_filament" title="Protein filament">filaments</a>. Each filament contains a <a href="/wiki/Capillary" title="Capillary">capillary</a> network that provides a large <a href="/wiki/Surface_area" title="Surface area">surface area</a> for exchanging <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>. Fish exchange gases by pulling oxygen-rich water through their mouths and pumping it over their gills. In some fish, capillary blood flows in the opposite direction to the water, causing <a href="/wiki/Countercurrent_exchange" title="Countercurrent exchange">countercurrent exchange</a>. The gills push the oxygen-poor water out through openings in the sides of the pharynx. Some fish, like <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lampreys</a>, possess multiple gill openings. However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bony_fish" title="Bony fish">bony fish</a> have a single gill opening on each side. This opening is hidden beneath a protective bony cover called an <a href="/wiki/Operculum_(fish)" title="Operculum (fish)">operculum</a>.
</p><p>Juvenile <a href="/wiki/Bichir" title="Bichir">bichirs</a> have external gills, a very primitive feature that they share with larval <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_breathing">Air breathing</span></h4>
<p>Fish from multiple groups can live out of the water for extended periods. <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_fish" title="Amphibious fish">Amphibious fish</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Mudskipper" title="Mudskipper">mudskipper</a> can live and move about on land for up to several days,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (December 2014)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Fish#Dubious" title="Talk:Fish">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> or live in stagnant or otherwise oxygen depleted water. Many such fish can breathe air via a variety of mechanisms. The skin of <a href="/wiki/Anguillidae" title="Anguillidae">anguillid eels</a> may absorb oxygen directly. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buccal_cavity" title="Buccal cavity">buccal cavity</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_eel" title="Electric eel">electric eel</a> may breathe air. Catfish of the families <a href="/wiki/Loricariidae" title="Loricariidae">Loricariidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callichthyidae" title="Callichthyidae">Callichthyidae</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scoloplacidae" title="Scoloplacidae">Scoloplacidae</a> absorb air through their digestive tracts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armbruster1998_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Armbruster1998-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">Lungfish</a>, with the exception of the <a href="/wiki/Australian_lungfish" title="Australian lungfish">Australian lungfish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bichir" title="Bichir">bichirs</a> have paired lungs similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a> and must surface to gulp fresh air through the mouth and pass spent air out through the gills. <a href="/wiki/Gar" title="Gar">Gar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bowfin" title="Bowfin">bowfin</a> have a vascularized swim bladder that functions in the same way. <a href="/wiki/Cypriniformes" title="Cypriniformes">Loaches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erythrinidae" title="Erythrinidae">trahiras</a>, and many <a href="/wiki/Catfish" title="Catfish">catfish</a> breathe by passing air through the gut. Mudskippers breathe by absorbing oxygen across the skin (similar to frogs). A number of fish have evolved so-called accessory breathing organs that extract oxygen from the air. Labyrinth fish (such as <a href="/wiki/Gourami" title="Gourami">gouramis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Betta" title="Betta">bettas</a>) have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labyrinth_(organ)" title="Labyrinth (organ)">labyrinth organ</a> above the gills that performs this function. A few other fish have structures resembling labyrinth organs in form and function, most notably <a href="/wiki/Snakehead_(fish)" title="Snakehead (fish)">snakeheads</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luciocephalidae" title="Luciocephalidae">pikeheads</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clariidae" title="Clariidae">Clariidae</a> catfish family.
</p><p>Breathing air is primarily of use to fish that inhabit shallow, seasonally variable waters where the water's oxygen concentration may seasonally decline. Fish dependent solely on dissolved oxygen, such as perch and <a href="/wiki/Cichlid" title="Cichlid">cichlids</a>, quickly suffocate, while air-breathers survive for much longer, in some cases in water that is little more than wet mud. At the most extreme, some air-breathing fish are able to survive in damp burrows for weeks without water, entering a state of <a href="/wiki/Aestivation" title="Aestivation">aestivation</a> (summertime hibernation) until water returns.
</p><p>Air breathing fish can be divided into obligate air breathers and facultative air breathers. Obligate air breathers, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_lungfish" title="African lungfish">African lungfish</a>, <i>must</i> breathe air periodically or they suffocate. Facultative air breathers, such as the catfish <i><a href="/wiki/Hypostomus_plecostomus" title="Hypostomus plecostomus">Hypostomus plecostomus</a></i>, only breathe air if they need to and will otherwise rely on their gills for oxygen. Most air breathing fish are facultative air breathers that avoid the energetic cost of rising to the surface and the fitness cost of exposure to surface predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armbruster1998_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Armbruster1998-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Circulation">Circulation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3872" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg/170px-Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg/255px-Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg/340px-Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Educational_toy" title="Educational toy">Didactic model</a> of a fish heart</div></div></div>
<p>Fish have a <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">closed-loop circulatory system</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> pumps the blood in a single loop throughout the body. In most fish, the heart consists of four parts, including two chambers and an entrance and exit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Setaro_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Setaro-39">[39]</a></sup> The first part is the <a href="/wiki/Sinus_venosus" title="Sinus venosus">sinus venosus</a>, a thin-walled sac that collects blood from the fish's <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a> before allowing it to flow to the second part, the <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)">atrium</a>, which is a large muscular chamber. The atrium serves as a one-way antechamber, sends blood to the third part, <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">ventricle</a>. The ventricle is another thick-walled, muscular chamber and it pumps the blood, first to the fourth part, <a href="/wiki/Bulbus_arteriosus" title="Bulbus arteriosus">bulbus arteriosus</a>, a large tube, and then out of the heart. The bulbus arteriosus connects to the <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">aorta</a>, through which blood flows to the gills for oxygenation.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digestion">Digestion</span></h3>
<p>Jaws allow fish to eat a wide variety of food, including plants and other organisms. Fish ingest food through the mouth and break it down in the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a>. In the stomach, food is further digested and, in many fish, processed in finger-shaped pouches called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyloric_caeca" title="Pyloric caeca">pyloric caeca</a>, which secrete digestive <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> and absorb nutrients. Organs such as the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">pancreas</a> add enzymes and various chemicals as the food moves through the digestive tract. The intestine completes the process of digestion and nutrient absorption.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Excretion">Excretion</span></h3>
<p>As with many aquatic animals, most fish release their nitrogenous wastes as <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>. Some of the wastes <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffuse</a> through the gills. Blood wastes are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filter_(chemistry)" title="Filter (chemistry)">filtered</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a>.
</p><p>Saltwater fish tend to lose water because of <a href="/wiki/Osmosis" title="Osmosis">osmosis</a>. Their kidneys return water to the body. The reverse happens in <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_fish" title="Freshwater fish">freshwater fish</a>: they tend to gain water osmotically. Their kidneys produce dilute urine for excretion. Some fish have specially adapted kidneys that vary in function, allowing them to move from freshwater to saltwater.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scales">Scales</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fish_scale" title="Fish scale">Fish scale</a></div>
<p>The scales of fish originate from the <a href="/wiki/Mesoderm" title="Mesoderm">mesoderm</a> (skin); they may be similar in structure to teeth.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sensory_and_nervous_system">Sensory and nervous system</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fish_brain.png"><img alt="Anatomical diagram showing the pairs of olfactory, telencephalon, and optic lobes, followed by the cerebellum and the mylencephalon" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1206" data-file-width="690" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Fish_brain.png/170px-Fish_brain.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Fish_brain.png/255px-Fish_brain.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Fish_brain.png/340px-Fish_brain.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fish_brain.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dorsal view of the brain of the <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_trout" title="Rainbow trout">rainbow trout</a></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_nervous_system">Central nervous system</span></h4>
<p>Fish typically have quite small brains relative to body size compared with other vertebrates, typically one-fifteenth the brain mass of a similarly sized bird or mammal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40">[40]</a></sup> However, some fish have relatively large brains, most notably <a href="/wiki/Mormyridae" title="Mormyridae">mormyrids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a>, which have brains about as massive relative to body weight as birds and <a href="/wiki/Marsupial" title="Marsupial">marsupials</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997191_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997191-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fish brains are divided into several regions. At the front are the <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">olfactory lobes</a>, a pair of structures that receive and process signals from the <a href="/wiki/Nostril" title="Nostril">nostrils</a> via the two <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_nerve" title="Olfactory nerve">olfactory nerves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40">[40]</a></sup> The olfactory lobes are very large in fish that hunt primarily by smell, such as hagfish, sharks, and catfish. Behind the olfactory lobes is the two-lobed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telencephalon" title="Telencephalon">telencephalon</a>, the structural equivalent to the <a href="/wiki/Cerebrum" title="Cerebrum">cerebrum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">higher vertebrates</a>. In fish the telencephalon is concerned mostly with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">olfaction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40">[40]</a></sup> Together these structures form the forebrain.
</p><p>Connecting the forebrain to the midbrain is the <a href="/wiki/Diencephalon" title="Diencephalon">diencephalon</a> (in the diagram, this structure is below the optic lobes and consequently not visible). The diencephalon performs functions associated with <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40">[40]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pineal_body" title="Pineal body">pineal body</a> lies just above the diencephalon. This structure detects light, maintains <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circadian" title="Circadian">circadian</a> rhythms, and controls color changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Midbrain" title="Midbrain">midbrain</a> (or mesencephalon) contains the two <a href="/wiki/Midbrain#Corpora_quadrigemina" title="Midbrain">optic lobes</a>. These are very large in species that hunt by sight, such as <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_trout" title="Rainbow trout">rainbow trout</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cichlid" title="Cichlid">cichlids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The hindbrain (or <a href="/wiki/Metencephalon" title="Metencephalon">metencephalon</a>) is particularly involved in swimming and balance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40">[40]</a></sup> The cerebellum is a single-lobed structure that is typically the biggest part of the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40">[40]</a></sup> Hagfish and <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lampreys</a> have relatively small cerebellae, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mormyrid" title="Mormyrid">mormyrid</a> cerebellum is massive and apparently involved in their <a href="/wiki/Electric_fish" title="Electric fish">electrical sense</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The brain stem (or <a href="/wiki/Myelencephalon" title="Myelencephalon">myelencephalon</a>) is the brain's posterior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40">[40]</a></sup> As well as controlling some muscles and body organs, in bony fish at least, the brain stem governs <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">respiration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osmoregulation" title="Osmoregulation">osmoregulation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sense_organs">Sense organs</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sensory_systems_in_fish" title="Sensory systems in fish">Sensory systems in fish</a></div>
<p>Most fish possess highly developed sense organs. Nearly all daylight fish have color vision that is at least as good as a human's (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_in_fishes" title="Vision in fishes">vision in fishes</a>). Many fish also have chemoreceptors that are responsible for extraordinary senses of taste and smell. Although they have ears, many fish may not hear very well. Most fish have sensitive receptors that form the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lateral_line_system" title="Lateral line system">lateral line system</a>, which detects gentle currents and vibrations, and senses the motion of nearby fish and prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encarta_99_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encarta_99-42">[42]</a></sup> The sense information obtained from the lateral line system can be considered both a sense of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Touch" title="Touch">touch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hearing" title="Hearing">hearing</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blind_cave_fish" title="Blind cave fish">Blind cave fish</a> navigate almost entirely through the sensations from their lateral line system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>  Some fish, such as catfish and sharks, have the <a href="/wiki/Ampullae_of_Lorenzini" title="Ampullae of Lorenzini">ampullae of Lorenzini</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electroreceptor" title="Electroreceptor">electroreceptors</a> that detect weak electric currents on the order of millivolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Other fish, like the South American electric fishes <a href="/wiki/Gymnotiformes" title="Gymnotiformes">Gymnotiformes</a>, can produce weak electric currents, which they use in navigation and social communication.
</p><p>Fish orient themselves using landmarks and may use mental maps based on multiple landmarks or symbols. Fish behavior in mazes reveals that they possess spatial memory and visual discrimination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vision">Vision</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_in_fishes" title="Vision in fishes">Vision in fishes</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Visual_system" title="Visual system">Vision</a> is an important <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_system" title="Sensory system">sensory system</a> for most species of fish. Fish eyes are similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal">terrestrial</a> <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> like <a href="/wiki/Bird_vision" title="Bird vision">birds</a> and mammals, but have a more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spherical" title="Spherical">spherical</a> <a href="/wiki/Lens_(anatomy)" title="Lens (anatomy)">lens</a>. Their <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retinas</a> generally have both <a href="/wiki/Rod_cell" title="Rod cell">rods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cone_cell" title="Cone cell">cones</a> (for <a href="/wiki/Scotopic_vision" title="Scotopic vision">scotopic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photopic_vision" title="Photopic vision">photopic vision</a>), and most species have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour_vision" title="Colour vision">colour vision</a>. Some fish can see <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> and some can see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polarized_light" title="Polarized light">polarized light</a>. Amongst <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jawless_fish" title="Jawless fish">jawless fish</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lamprey</a> has well-developed eyes, while the <a href="/wiki/Hagfish" title="Hagfish">hagfish</a> has only primitive <a href="/wiki/Eyespot_(mimicry)" title="Eyespot (mimicry)">eyespots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Fish vision shows <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptation</a> to their visual environment, for example <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_sea_fish" title="Deep sea fish">deep sea fishes</a> have eyes suited to the dark environment.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hearing">Hearing</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hearing_in_fish" title="Hearing in fish">Hearing in fish</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hearing_(sense)" title="Hearing (sense)">Hearing</a> is an important sensory system for most species of fish. Fish sense sound using their <a href="/wiki/Lateral_line" title="Lateral line">lateral lines</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Otolith" title="Otolith">ears</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognition">Cognition</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fish_intelligence" title="Fish intelligence">Fish intelligence</a></div>
<p>New research has expanded preconceptions about the cognitive capacities of fish. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manta_rays" title="Manta rays">manta rays</a> have exhibited behavior linked to <a href="/wiki/Self-awareness" title="Self-awareness">self-awareness</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mirror_test" title="Mirror test">mirror test</a> cases. Placed in front of a mirror, individual rays engaged in contingency testing, that is, repetitive behavior aiming to check whether their reflection's behavior mimics their body movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wrasses" title="Wrasses">Wrasses</a> have also passed the mirror test in a 2018 scientific study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cases of tool use have also been noticed, notably in the <a href="/wiki/Choerodon" title="Choerodon">Choerodon</a> family, in <a href="/wiki/Archerfish" title="Archerfish">archerfish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_cod" title="Atlantic cod">Atlantic cod</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Capacity_for_pain">Capacity for pain</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pain_in_fish" title="Pain in fish">Pain in fish</a></div>
<p>Experiments done by William Tavolga provide evidence that fish have <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a> and fear responses. For instance, in Tavolga's experiments, <a href="/wiki/Batrachoididae" title="Batrachoididae">toadfish</a> grunted when electrically shocked and over time they came to grunt at the mere sight of an electrode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2003, Scottish scientists at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> and the Roslin Institute concluded that rainbow trout exhibit behaviors often associated with <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a> in other animals. <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">Bee</a> <a href="/wiki/Venom" title="Venom">venom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a> injected into the lips resulted in fish rocking their bodies and rubbing their lips along the sides and floors of their tanks, which the researchers concluded were attempts to relieve pain, similar to what mammals would do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grandin183_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-grandin183-53">[53]</a></sup> Neurons fired in a pattern resembling human neuronal patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grandin183_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-grandin183-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Professor James D. Rose of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wyoming" title="University of Wyoming">University of Wyoming</a> claimed the study was flawed since it did not provide proof that fish possess "conscious awareness, particularly a kind of awareness that is meaningfully like ours".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Rose argues that since fish brains are so different from human brains, fish are probably not conscious in the manner humans are, so that reactions similar to human reactions to pain instead have other causes. Rose had published a study a year earlier arguing that fish cannot feel pain because their brains lack a <a href="/wiki/Neocortex" title="Neocortex">neocortex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> However, animal behaviorist <a href="/wiki/Temple_Grandin" title="Temple Grandin">Temple Grandin</a> argues that fish could still have consciousness without a neocortex because "different species can use different brain structures and systems to handle the same functions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grandin183_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-grandin183-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Animal welfare advocates raise concerns about the possible <a href="/wiki/Suffering" title="Suffering">suffering</a> of fish caused by angling. Some countries, such as Germany, have banned specific types of fishing, and the British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RSPCA" title="RSPCA">RSPCA</a> now formally prosecutes individuals who are cruel to fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Emotion">Emotion</span></h4>
<p>In 2019, scientists have shown that members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monogamous" title="Monogamous">monogamous</a> species <i><a href="/wiki/Amatitlania_siquia" title="Amatitlania siquia">Amatitlania siquia</a></i> exhibit pessimistic behavior when they are prevented from being with their partner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Muscular_system">Muscular system</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fish_locomotion" title="Fish locomotion">Fish locomotion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lampanyctodes_hectoris_(Hector%27s_lanternfish).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="288" data-file-width="747" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Lampanyctodes_hectoris_%28Hector%27s_lanternfish%29.svg/330px-Lampanyctodes_hectoris_%28Hector%27s_lanternfish%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Lampanyctodes_hectoris_%28Hector%27s_lanternfish%29.svg/495px-Lampanyctodes_hectoris_%28Hector%27s_lanternfish%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Lampanyctodes_hectoris_%28Hector%27s_lanternfish%29.svg/660px-Lampanyctodes_hectoris_%28Hector%27s_lanternfish%29.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lampanyctodes_hectoris_(Hector%27s_lanternfish).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The anatomy of <i>Lampanyctodes hectoris</i> (1) operculum (gill cover), (2) lateral line, (3) dorsal fin, (4) fat fin, (5) caudal peduncle, (6) caudal fin, (7) anal fin, (8) photophores, (9) pelvic fins (paired), (10) pectoral fins (paired)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Swim_bladder.jpg"><img alt="Photo of white bladder that consists of a rectangular section and a banana-shaped section connected by a much thinner element" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="235" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="65" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Swim_bladder.jpg/220px-Swim_bladder.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Swim_bladder.jpg/330px-Swim_bladder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Swim_bladder.jpg/440px-Swim_bladder.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Swim_bladder.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Swim bladder of a rudd (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scardinius_erythrophthalmus" title="Scardinius erythrophthalmus">Scardinius erythrophthalmus</a></i>)</div></div></div>
<p>Most fish move by alternately contracting paired sets of muscles on either side of the backbone. These contractions form S-shaped curves that move down the body. As each curve reaches the back fin, backward force is applied to the water, and in conjunction with the fins, moves the fish forward. The fish's fins function like an airplane's flaps. Fins also increase the tail's surface area, increasing speed. The streamlined body of the fish decreases the amount of friction from the water. Since body tissue is denser than water, fish must compensate for the difference or they will sink. Many bony fish have an internal organ called a <a href="/wiki/Swim_bladder" title="Swim bladder">swim bladder</a> that adjusts their buoyancy through manipulation of gases.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Endothermy">Endothermy</span></h4>
<p>Although most fish are exclusively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ectothermic" title="Ectothermic">ectothermic</a>, there are exceptions. The only known bony fishes (infraclass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teleostei" title="Teleostei">Teleostei</a>) that exhibit <a href="/wiki/Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded">endothermy</a> are in the suborder <a href="/wiki/Scombroidei" title="Scombroidei">Scombroidei</a> – which includes the <a href="/wiki/Billfish" title="Billfish">billfishes</a>, tunas, and the <a href="/wiki/Butterfly_kingfish" title="Butterfly kingfish">butterfly kingfish</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> species of mackerel<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Block1993_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Block1993-58">[58]</a></sup> – and also the <a href="/wiki/Lampris_guttatus" title="Lampris guttatus">opah</a>. The opah, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lampriform" title="Lampriform">lampriform</a>, was demonstrated in 2015 to utilize "whole-body endothermy", generating heat with its swimming muscles to warm its body while countercurrent exchange (as in respiration) minimizes heat loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> It is able to actively hunt prey such as squid and swim for long distances due to the ability to warm its entire body, including its heart,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> which is a trait typically found in only mammals and birds (in the form of <a href="/wiki/Homeothermy" title="Homeothermy">homeothermy</a>). In the cartilaginous fishes (class <a href="/wiki/Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes">Chondrichthyes</a>), sharks of the families <a href="/wiki/Lamnidae" title="Lamnidae">Lamnidae</a> (porbeagle, mackerel, salmon, and great white sharks) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alopiidae" title="Alopiidae">Alopiidae</a> (thresher sharks) exhibit endothermy. The degree of endothermy varies from the billfishes, which warm only their eyes and brain, to the <a href="/wiki/Bluefin_tuna" title="Bluefin tuna">bluefin tuna</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porbeagle_shark" title="Porbeagle shark">porbeagle shark</a>, which maintain body temperatures in excess of 20 °C (68 °F) above ambient water temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Block1993_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Block1993-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Endothermy, though metabolically costly, is thought to provide advantages such as increased muscle strength, higher rates of central <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> processing, and higher rates of <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestion</a>.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Reproductive_method"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_system">Reproductive system</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fish_reproduction" title="Fish reproduction">Fish reproduction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spawn_(biology)" title="Spawn (biology)">Spawn (biology)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2800" data-file-width="2240" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ovary of fish (Corumbatá)</div></div></div>
<p>Fish reproductive organs include <a href="/wiki/Testicle" title="Testicle">testicles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ovary" title="Ovary">ovaries</a>. In most species, gonads are paired organs of similar size, which can be partially or totally fused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guimaraes_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guimaraes-61">[61]</a></sup> There may also be a range of secondary organs that increase reproductive fitness.
</p><p>In terms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spermatogonia" title="Spermatogonia">spermatogonia</a> distribution, the structure of <a href="/wiki/Teleost" title="Teleost">teleosts</a> testes has two types: in the most common, spermatogonia occur all along the <a href="/wiki/Seminiferous_tubule" title="Seminiferous tubule">seminiferous tubules</a>, while in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/atherinomorph" title="wikt:atherinomorph">atherinomorph</a> fish they are confined to the <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location#Proximal_and_distal" title="Anatomical terms of location">distal</a> portion of these structures. Fish can present cystic or semi-cystic <a href="/wiki/Spermatogenesis" title="Spermatogenesis">spermatogenesis</a> in relation to the release phase of germ cells in cysts to the seminiferous tubules <a href="/wiki/Lumen_(anatomy)" title="Lumen (anatomy)">lumen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guimaraes_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guimaraes-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fish ovaries may be of three types: gymnovarian, secondary gymnovarian or cystovarian. In the first type, the <a href="/wiki/Oocyte" title="Oocyte">oocytes</a> are released directly into the <a href="/wiki/Coelom" title="Coelom">coelomic</a> cavity and then enter the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ostium_of_Fallopian_tube" title="Ostium of Fallopian tube">ostium</a>, then through the <a href="/wiki/Oviduct" title="Oviduct">oviduct</a> and are eliminated. Secondary gymnovarian ovaries shed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovum" title="Ovum">ova</a> into the coelom from which they go directly into the oviduct. In the third type, the oocytes are conveyed to the exterior through the oviduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brito_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brito-62">[62]</a></sup> Gymnovaries are the primitive condition found in <a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">lungfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sturgeon" title="Sturgeon">sturgeon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bowfin" title="Bowfin">bowfin</a>. Cystovaries characterize most teleosts, where the ovary lumen has continuity with the oviduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guimaraes_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guimaraes-61">[61]</a></sup> Secondary gymnovaries are found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salmonid" title="Salmonid">salmonids</a> and a few other teleosts.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oogonia" title="Oogonia">Oogonia</a> development in teleosts fish varies according to the group, and the determination of oogenesis dynamics allows the understanding of maturation and fertilization processes. Changes in the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a>, ooplasm, and the surrounding layers characterize the oocyte maturation process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guimaraes_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-Guimaraes-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Postovulatory <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_follicle" title="Ovarian follicle">follicles</a> are structures formed after oocyte release; they do not have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endocrine" title="Endocrine">endocrine</a> function, present a wide irregular lumen, and are rapidly reabsorbed in a process involving the <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a> of follicular cells. A degenerative process called <a href="/wiki/Follicular_atresia" title="Follicular atresia">follicular atresia</a> reabsorbs vitellogenic oocytes not spawned. This process can also occur, but less frequently, in oocytes in other development stages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guimaraes_61-4"><a href="#cite_note-Guimaraes-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some fish, like the <a href="/wiki/California_sheephead" title="California sheephead">California sheephead</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite">hermaphrodites</a>, having both testes and ovaries either at different phases in their life cycle or, as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamlet_(fish)" title="Hamlet (fish)">hamlets</a>, have them simultaneously.
</p><p>Over 97% of all known fish are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oviparous" title="Oviparous">oviparous</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-63">[63]</a></sup> that is, the eggs develop outside the mother's body. Examples of oviparous fish include <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goldfish" title="Goldfish">goldfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cichlid" title="Cichlid">cichlids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eel" title="Eel">eels</a>. In the majority of these species, fertilisation takes place outside the mother's body, with the male and female fish shedding their <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gametes</a> into the surrounding water. However, a few oviparous fish practice internal fertilization, with the male using some sort of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ichthyology_terms#I" title="Ichthyology terms">intromittent organ</a> to deliver sperm into the genital opening of the female, most notably the oviparous sharks, such as the <a href="/wiki/Horn_shark" title="Horn shark">horn shark</a>, and oviparous rays, such as <a href="/wiki/Skate_(fish)" title="Skate (fish)">skates</a>. In these cases, the male is equipped with a pair of modified <a href="/wiki/Pelvic_fin" title="Pelvic fin">pelvic fins</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Clasper" title="Clasper">claspers</a>.
</p><p>Marine fish can produce high numbers of eggs which are often released into the open water column. The eggs have an average diameter of 1 millimetre (0.04 in).
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
