</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The newly hatched young of oviparous fish are called <a href="/wiki/Ichthyoplankton" title="Ichthyoplankton">larvae</a>. They are usually poorly formed, carry a large <a href="/wiki/Yolk_sac" title="Yolk sac">yolk sac</a> (for nourishment), and are very different in appearance from juvenile and adult specimens. The larval period in oviparous fish is relatively short (usually only several weeks), and larvae rapidly grow and change appearance and structure (a process termed <a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis" title="Metamorphosis">metamorphosis</a>) to become juveniles. During this transition larvae must switch from their yolk sac to feeding on <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> prey, a process which depends on typically inadequate zooplankton density, starving many larvae.
</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovoviviparous" title="Ovoviviparous">ovoviviparous</a> fish the eggs develop inside the mother's body after internal fertilization but receive little or no nourishment directly from the mother, depending instead on the <a href="/wiki/Yolk" title="Yolk">yolk</a>. Each embryo develops in its own egg. Familiar examples of ovoviviparous fish include <a href="/wiki/Guppy" title="Guppy">guppies</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angel_shark" title="Angel shark">angel sharks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coelacanth" title="Coelacanth">coelacanths</a>.
</p><p>Some species of fish are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viviparous" title="Viviparous">viviparous</a>. In such species the mother retains the eggs and nourishes the embryos. Typically, viviparous fish have a structure analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a> seen in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placental_mammals" title="Placental mammals">mammals</a> connecting the mother's blood supply with that of the embryo. Examples of viviparous fish include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embiotocidae" title="Embiotocidae">surf-perches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goodeidae" title="Goodeidae">splitfins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lemon_shark" title="Lemon shark">lemon shark</a>. Some viviparous fish exhibit <a href="/wiki/Oophagy" title="Oophagy">oophagy</a>, in which the developing embryos eat other eggs produced by the mother. This has been observed primarily among sharks, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shortfin_mako" title="Shortfin mako">shortfin mako</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porbeagle" title="Porbeagle">porbeagle</a>, but is known for a few bony fish as well, such as the <a href="/wiki/Halfbeak" title="Halfbeak">halfbeak</a> <i>Nomorhamphus ebrardtii</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meisner_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meisner-64">[64]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intrauterine_cannibalism" title="Intrauterine cannibalism">Intrauterine cannibalism</a> is an even more unusual mode of vivipary, in which the largest embryos eat weaker and smaller siblings. This behavior is also most commonly found among sharks, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grey_nurse_shark" title="Grey nurse shark">grey nurse shark</a>, but has also been reported for <i>Nomorhamphus ebrardtii</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meisner_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meisner-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fishkeeping" title="Fishkeeping">Aquarists</a> commonly refer to ovoviviparous and viviparous fish as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Livebearer" title="Livebearer">livebearers</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Acoustic_communication">Acoustic communication</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acoustic_communication_in_aquatic_animals" title="Acoustic communication in aquatic animals">Acoustic communication in aquatic animals</a></div>
<p>Acoustic communication in fish involves the transmission of acoustic signals from one individual of a species to another. The production of sounds as a means of communication among fish is most often used in the context of feeding, aggression or courtship behaviour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bioacoustics_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bioacoustics-3">[3]</a></sup> The sounds emitted can vary depending on the species and stimulus involved. Fish can produce either stridulatory sounds by moving components of the skeletal system, or can produce non-stridulatory sounds by manipulating specialized organs such as the swimbladder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Journal-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stridulatory">Stridulatory</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg/220px-French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg/330px-French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg/440px-French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>French grunts – <i><a href="/wiki/Haemulon_flavolineatum" title="Haemulon flavolineatum">Haemulon flavolineatum</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>There are some species of fish that can produce sounds by rubbing or grinding their bones together. These noises produced by bone-on-bone interactions are known as 'stridulatory sounds'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Journal-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>An example of this is seen in <i><a href="/wiki/Haemulon_flavolineatum" title="Haemulon flavolineatum">Haemulon flavolineatum</a></i>, a species commonly referred to as the 'French grunt fish', as it produces a grunting noise by grinding its teeth together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-Journal-65">[65]</a></sup> This behaviour is most pronounced when the <i>H. flavolineatum</i> is in distress situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-Journal-65">[65]</a></sup> The grunts produced by this species of fishes generate a frequency of approximately 700 Hz, and last approximately 47 milliseconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_65-4"><a href="#cite_note-Journal-65">[65]</a></sup> The <i>H. flavolineatum</i> does not emit sounds with frequencies greater than 1000 Hz, and does not detect sounds that have frequencies greater than 1050 Hz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_65-5"><a href="#cite_note-Journal-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a study conducted by Oliveira et al. (2014), the longsnout seahorse, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hippocampus_reidi" title="Hippocampus reidi">Hippocampus reidi</a></i>, was recorded producing two different categories of sounds; 'clicks' and 'growls'. The sounds emitted by the <i>H. reidi</i> are accomplished by rubbing their coronet bone across the grooved section of their neurocranium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> 'Clicking' sounds were found to be primarily produced during courtship and feeding, and the frequencies of clicks were within the range of 50 Hz-800 Hz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zoology_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zoology-67">[67]</a></sup> The frequencies were noted to be on the higher end of the range during spawning periods, when the female and male fishes were less than fifteen centimeters apart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zoology_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zoology-67">[67]</a></sup> Growl sounds were produced when the <i>H. reidi</i> encountered stressful situations, such as handling by researchers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zoology_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zoology-67">[67]</a></sup> The 'growl' sounds consist of a series of sound pulses and are emitted simultaneously with body vibrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zoology_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zoology-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-stridulatory">Non-stridulatory</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:C(flipped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1532" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/C%28flipped%29.jpg/220px-C%28flipped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/C%28flipped%29.jpg/330px-C%28flipped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/C%28flipped%29.jpg/440px-C%28flipped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:C(flipped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Oyster toadfish</div></div></div></div>
<p>Some fish species create noise by engaging specialized muscles that contract and cause swimbladder vibrations.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Oyster_toadfish" title="Oyster toadfish">Oyster toadfish</a> produce loud grunting sounds by contracting muscles located along the sides of their swim bladder, known as sonic muscles <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Experimental_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Experimental-68">[68]</a></sup> Female and male toadfishes emit short-duration grunts, often as a fright response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In addition to short-duration grunts, male toadfishes produce "boat whistle calls".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plos_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-plos-70">[70]</a></sup> These calls are longer in duration, lower in frequency, and are primarily used to attract mates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plos_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-plos-70">[70]</a></sup> The sounds emitted by the <i>O. tao</i> have frequency range of 140 Hz to 260 Hz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plos_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-plos-70">[70]</a></sup> The frequencies of the calls depend on the rate at which the sonic muscles contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Experimental_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Experimental-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>The red drum, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sciaenops_ocellatus" title="Sciaenops ocellatus">Sciaenops ocellatus</a></i>, produces drumming sounds by vibrating its swimbladder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elsevier-72">[72]</a></sup> Vibrations are caused by the rapid contraction of sonic muscles that surround the dorsal aspect of the swimbladder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elsevier-72">[72]</a></sup> These vibrations result in repeated sounds with frequencies that range from 100 to &gt;200 Hz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-Elsevier-72">[72]</a></sup> The <i>S. ocellatus</i> can produce different calls depending on the stimuli involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_72-3"><a href="#cite_note-Elsevier-72">[72]</a></sup> The sounds created in courtship situations are different from those made during distressing events such as predatorial attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_72-4"><a href="#cite_note-Elsevier-72">[72]</a></sup> Unlike the males of the <i>S. ocellatus</i> species, the females of this species do not produce sounds and lack sound-producing (sonic) muscles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_72-5"><a href="#cite_note-Elsevier-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">Diseases</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_diseases_and_parasites" title="Fish diseases and parasites">Fish diseases and parasites</a></div>
<p>Like other animals, fish suffer from diseases and parasites. To prevent disease they have a variety of defenses. <i>Non-specific</i> defenses include the skin and scales, as well as the mucus layer secreted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidermis_(skin)" title="Epidermis (skin)">epidermis</a> that traps and inhibits the growth of <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>. If <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a> breach these defenses, fish can develop an <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammatory response</a> that increases blood flow to the infected region and delivers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_blood_cells" title="White blood cells">white blood cells</a> that attempt to destroy pathogens. Specific defenses respond to particular pathogens recognised by the fish's body, i.e., an <a href="/wiki/Immune_response" title="Immune response">immune response</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199795–96_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199795–96-73">[73]</a></sup> In recent years, <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a> have become widely used in aquaculture and also with ornamental fish, for example <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Furunculosis" title="Furunculosis">furunculosis</a> vaccines in farmed <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koi_herpes_virus" title="Koi herpes virus">koi herpes virus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Koi" title="Koi">koi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some species use <a href="/wiki/Cleaner_fish" title="Cleaner fish">cleaner fish</a> to remove external parasites. The best known of these are the <a href="/wiki/Bluestreak_cleaner_wrasse" title="Bluestreak cleaner wrasse">bluestreak cleaner wrasses</a> of the genus <i>Labroides</i> found on <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> oceans. These small fish maintain so-called "cleaning stations" where other fish congregate and perform specific movements to attract the attention of the cleaners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997380_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997380-76">[76]</a></sup> Cleaning behaviors have been observed in a number of fish groups, including an interesting case between two cichlids of the same genus, <i><a href="/wiki/Orange_chromide" title="Orange chromide">Etroplus maculatus</a></i>, the cleaner, and the much larger <i>Etroplus suratensis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immune_system">Immune system</span></h3>
<p>Immune organs vary by type of fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jawless_fish" title="Jawless fish">jawless fish</a> (lampreys and hagfish), true <a href="/wiki/Lymph" title="Lymph">lymphoid</a> organs are absent. These fish rely on regions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lymphoid_tissue" title="Lymphoid tissue">lymphoid tissue</a> within other organs to produce immune cells. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erythrocytes" title="Erythrocytes">erythrocytes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrophages" title="Macrophages">macrophages</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_cells" title="Plasma cells">plasma cells</a> are produced in the anterior kidney (or <a href="/wiki/Pronephros" title="Pronephros">pronephros</a>) and some areas of the gut (where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Granulocytes" title="Granulocytes">granulocytes</a> mature.) They resemble primitive <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a> in hagfish. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartilaginous_fish" title="Cartilaginous fish">Cartilaginous fish</a> (sharks and rays) have a more advanced immune system. They have three specialized organs that are unique to <a href="/wiki/Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes">Chondrichthyes</a>; the epigonal organs (lymphoid tissue similar to mammalian bone) that surround the gonads, the <a href="/wiki/Leydig%27s_organ" title="Leydig's organ">Leydig's organ</a> within the walls of their esophagus, and a <a href="/wiki/Spiral_valve" title="Spiral valve">spiral valve</a> in their intestine. These organs house typical immune cells (granulocytes, lymphocytes and plasma cells). They also possess an identifiable <a href="/wiki/Thymus" title="Thymus">thymus</a> and a well-developed <a href="/wiki/Spleen" title="Spleen">spleen</a> (their most important immune organ) where various <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">lymphocytes</a>, plasma cells and macrophages develop and are stored. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chondrostean" title="Chondrostean">Chondrostean</a> fish (sturgeons, paddlefish, and bichirs) possess a major site for the production of granulocytes within a mass that is associated with the <a href="/wiki/Meninges" title="Meninges">meninges</a> (membranes surrounding the central nervous system.) Their heart is frequently covered with tissue that contains lymphocytes, <a href="/wiki/Reticular_cell" title="Reticular cell">reticular cells</a> and a small number of <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a>. The chondrostean kidney is an important <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemopoietic" title="Hemopoietic">hemopoietic</a> organ; where erythrocytes, granulocytes, lymphocytes and macrophages develop.
</p><p>Like chondrostean fish, the major immune tissues of bony fish (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teleostei" title="Teleostei">teleostei</a>) include the kidney (especially the anterior kidney), which houses many different immune cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> In addition, teleost fish possess a thymus, spleen and scattered immune areas within mucosal tissues (e.g. in the skin, gills, gut and gonads). Much like the mammalian immune system, teleost erythrocytes, neutrophils and granulocytes are believed to reside in the spleen whereas lymphocytes are the major cell type found in the thymus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In 2006, a lymphatic system similar to that in mammals was described in one species of teleost fish, the <a href="/wiki/Zebrafish" title="Zebrafish">zebrafish</a>. Although not confirmed as yet, this system presumably will be where naive (unstimulated) <a href="/wiki/T_cell" title="T cell">T cells</a> accumulate while waiting to encounter an <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>B and T lymphocytes bearing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin" title="Immunoglobulin">immunoglobulins</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T_cell_receptor" title="T cell receptor">T cell receptors</a>, respectively, are found in all jawed fishes. Indeed, the <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immune_system" title="Adaptive immune system">adaptive immune system</a> as a whole <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immune_system#Evolution" title="Adaptive immune system">evolved</a> in an ancestor of all jawed vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span></h2>
<p>The 2006 <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">IUCN</a> <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">Red List</a> names 1,173 fish species that are threatened with extinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Included are species such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gadus_morhua" title="Gadus morhua">Atlantic cod</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyprinodon_diabolis" title="Cyprinodon diabolis">Devil's Hole pupfish</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coelacanth" title="Coelacanth">coelacanths</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Great_white_shark" title="Great white shark">great white sharks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Because fish live underwater they are more difficult to study than terrestrial animals and plants, and information about fish populations is often lacking. However, freshwater fish seem particularly threatened because they often live in relatively small water bodies. For example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devil%27s_Hole_pupfish" title="Devil's Hole pupfish">Devil's Hole pupfish</a> occupies only a single 3 by 6 metres (10 by 20 ft) pool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997449–450_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997449–450-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overfishing">Overfishing</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg"><img alt="Photo of shark in profile surrounded by other, much smaller fish in bright sunlight" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1074" data-file-width="2331" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg/220px-Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg/330px-Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg/440px-Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Whale_shark" title="Whale shark">Whale sharks</a>, the largest species of fish, are classified as <a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">vulnerable</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">Overfishing</a></div>
<p>Overfishing is a major threat to edible fish such as cod and <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Overfishing eventually causes <a href="/wiki/Population_dynamics_of_fisheries" title="Population dynamics of fisheries">population</a> (known as <a href="/wiki/Fish_stocks" title="Fish stocks">stock</a>) collapse because the survivors cannot produce enough young to replace those removed. Such <b>commercial extinction</b> does not mean that the species is extinct, merely that it can no longer sustain a fishery.
</p><p>One well-studied example of fishery collapse is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_sardine" title="Pacific sardine">Pacific sardine</a> <i>Sadinops sagax caerulues</i> fishery off the California coast. From a 1937 peak of 790,000 long tons (800,000 t) the catch steadily declined to only 24,000 long tons (24,000 t) in 1968, after which the fishery was no longer economically viable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997462_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997462-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main tension between <a href="/wiki/Fisheries_science" title="Fisheries science">fisheries science</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Fishing_industry" title="Fishing industry">fishing industry</a> is that the two groups have different views on the resiliency of fisheries to intensive fishing. In places such as Scotland, Newfoundland, and Alaska the fishing industry is a major employer, so governments are predisposed to support it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> On the other hand, scientists and conservationists push for stringent protection, warning that many stocks could be wiped out within fifty years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat_destruction">Habitat destruction</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_fishing" title="Environmental impact of fishing">Environmental impact of fishing</a></div>
<p>A key stress on both freshwater and marine ecosystems is habitat degradation including <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, the building of dams, removal of water for use by humans, and the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">exotic</a> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997463_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997463-97">[97]</a></sup> An example of a fish that has become endangered because of habitat change is the <a href="/wiki/Pallid_sturgeon" title="Pallid sturgeon">pallid sturgeon</a>, a North American freshwater fish that lives in rivers damaged by human activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exotic_species">Exotic species</span></h3>
<p>Introduction of <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">non-native</a> species has occurred in many habitats. One of the best studied examples is the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Nile_perch" title="Nile perch">Nile perch</a> into <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> in the 1960s. Nile perch gradually exterminated the lake's 500 <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> <a href="/wiki/Cichlid" title="Cichlid">cichlid</a> species. Some of them survive now in captive breeding programmes, but others are probably extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carp" title="Carp">Carp</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Channidae" title="Channidae">snakeheads</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tilapia" title="Tilapia">tilapia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perch" title="Perch">European perch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brown_trout" title="Brown trout">brown trout</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_trout" title="Rainbow trout">rainbow trout</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sea_lamprey" title="Sea lamprey">sea lampreys</a> are other examples of fish that have caused problems by being introduced into alien environments.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Importance_to_humans">Importance to humans</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic">Economic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Fishing_industry" title="Fishing industry">Fishing industry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">Aquaculture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fish_farming" title="Fish farming">Fish farming</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Community_fish-farming_ponds_in_the_rural_town_of_Masi_Manimba,_DRC_(7609946524).jpg"><img alt="A row of square artificial ponds, with trees on either side" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Community_fish-farming_ponds_in_the_rural_town_of_Masi_Manimba%2C_DRC_%287609946524%29.jpg/220px-Community_fish-farming_ponds_in_the_rural_town_of_Masi_Manimba%2C_DRC_%287609946524%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Community_fish-farming_ponds_in_the_rural_town_of_Masi_Manimba%2C_DRC_%287609946524%29.jpg/330px-Community_fish-farming_ponds_in_the_rural_town_of_Masi_Manimba%2C_DRC_%287609946524%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Community_fish-farming_ponds_in_the_rural_town_of_Masi_Manimba%2C_DRC_%287609946524%29.jpg/440px-Community_fish-farming_ponds_in_the_rural_town_of_Masi_Manimba%2C_DRC_%287609946524%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Community_fish-farming_ponds_in_the_rural_town_of_Masi_Manimba,_DRC_(7609946524).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>These fish-farming ponds were created as a <a href="/wiki/Cooperative" title="Cooperative">cooperative</a> project in a rural village.</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout history, humans have utilized <a href="/wiki/Fish_as_food" title="Fish as food">fish as a food source</a>. Historically and today, most fish <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> has come by means of catching wild fish. However, aquaculture, or fish farming, which has been practiced since about 3,500 BCE. in China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> is becoming increasingly important in many nations. Overall, about one-sixth of the world's protein is estimated to be provided by fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helfman_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Helfman-102">[102]</a></sup> That proportion is considerably elevated in some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_nation" title="Developing nation">developing nations</a> and regions heavily dependent on the sea. In a similar manner, fish have been tied to trade.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oulu_Market_Hall_20170617_04.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2477" data-file-width="3300" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Oulu_Market_Hall_20170617_04.jpg/220px-Oulu_Market_Hall_20170617_04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Oulu_Market_Hall_20170617_04.jpg/330px-Oulu_Market_Hall_20170617_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Oulu_Market_Hall_20170617_04.jpg/440px-Oulu_Market_Hall_20170617_04.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oulu_Market_Hall_20170617_04.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fish counter display at the <a href="/wiki/Oulu_Market_Hall" title="Oulu Market Hall">Oulu Market Hall</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oulu" title="Oulu">Oulu</a>, Finland.</div></div></div>
<p>Catching fish for the purpose of food or sport is known as <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishing</a>, while the organized effort by humans to catch fish is called a <a href="/wiki/Fishery" title="Fishery">fishery</a>. Fisheries are a huge global business and provide income for millions of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helfman_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Helfman-102">[102]</a></sup> The annual yield from all fisheries worldwide is about 154 million tons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> with popular species including <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cod" title="Cod">cod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anchovy" title="Anchovy">anchovy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flounder" title="Flounder">flounder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>. However, the term fishery is broadly applied, and includes more organisms than just fish, such as <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">mollusks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, which are often called "fish" when used as food.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreation">Recreation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vendor_in_Fish_Market_-_Sylhet_-_Bangladesh_(12968716714).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Vendor_in_Fish_Market_-_Sylhet_-_Bangladesh_%2812968716714%29.jpg/220px-Vendor_in_Fish_Market_-_Sylhet_-_Bangladesh_%2812968716714%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Vendor_in_Fish_Market_-_Sylhet_-_Bangladesh_%2812968716714%29.jpg/330px-Vendor_in_Fish_Market_-_Sylhet_-_Bangladesh_%2812968716714%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Vendor_in_Fish_Market_-_Sylhet_-_Bangladesh_%2812968716714%29.jpg/440px-Vendor_in_Fish_Market_-_Sylhet_-_Bangladesh_%2812968716714%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vendor_in_Fish_Market_-_Sylhet_-_Bangladesh_(12968716714).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Bengalis" title="Bengalis">Bengali</a> fish vendor</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Fishkeeping" title="Fishkeeping">Fishkeeping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recreational_fishing" title="Recreational fishing">Recreational fishing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Angling" title="Angling">Angling</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fishkeeping">Fishkeeping</span></h4>
<p>Fish have been recognized as a source of beauty for almost as long as used for food, appearing in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cave_art" title="Cave art">cave art</a>, being raised as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ornamental_fish" title="Ornamental fish">ornamental fish</a> in ponds, and displayed in <a href="/wiki/Aquarium" title="Aquarium">aquariums</a> in homes, offices, or public settings.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreational_fishing">Recreational fishing</span></h4>
<p><i>Recreational fishing</i> is fishing primarily for pleasure or competition; it can be contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Commercial_fishing" title="Commercial fishing">commercial fishing</a>, which is fishing for profit, or <a href="/wiki/Artisanal_fishing" title="Artisanal fishing">artisanal fishing</a>, which is fishing primarily for food. The most common form of recreational fishing is done with a <a href="/wiki/Fishing_rod" title="Fishing rod">rod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fishing_reel" title="Fishing reel">reel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fishing_line" title="Fishing line">line</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish_hook" title="Fish hook">hooks</a>, and any one of a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Bait_(luring_substance)" title="Bait (luring substance)">baits</a>.  Recreational fishing is particularly popular in North America and Europe and state, provincial, and federal government agencies actively management target fish species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Angling is a method of fishing, specifically the practice of catching fish by means of an "angle" (hook). Anglers must select the right hook, <a href="/wiki/Cast_net" title="Cast net">cast</a> accurately, and retrieve at the right speed while considering water and weather conditions, species, fish response, time of the day, and other factors.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fish_in_culture" title="Fish in culture">Fish in culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Matsya_painting.jpg"><img alt="Photo of painting showing blue-skinned, 4-armed upper body of man standing in the opened mouth of a fish with bent tail with other, paler men facing him with hands raised together" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="977" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Matsya_painting.jpg/170px-Matsya_painting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Matsya_painting.jpg/255px-Matsya_painting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Matsya_painting.jpg/340px-Matsya_painting.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Matsya_painting.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Avatar" title="Avatar">Avatar</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Matsya" title="Matsya">Matsya</a></div></div></div>
<p>Fish themes have symbolic significance in many religions. In ancient <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, fish offerings were made to the gods from the very earliest times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-106">[106]</a></sup> Fish were also a major symbol of <a href="/wiki/Enki" title="Enki">Enki</a>, the god of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-106">[106]</a></sup> Fish frequently appear as filling motifs in <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_seal" title="Cylinder seal">cylinder seals</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Babylonian_dynasty" title="First Babylonian dynasty">Old Babylonian</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1830 BC – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1531 BC) and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian</a> (911–609 BC) periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-106">[106]</a></sup> Starting during the <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassite Period</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1600 BC – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1155 BC) and lasting until the early <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persian Period</a> (550–30 BC), healers and exorcists dressed in ritual garb resembling the bodies of fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-106">[106]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Period</a> (312–63 BC), the legendary Babylonian <a href="/wiki/Culture_hero" title="Culture hero">culture hero</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oannes_(mythology)" title="Oannes (mythology)">Oannes</a>, described by <a href="/wiki/Berossus" title="Berossus">Berossus</a>, was said to have dressed in the skin of a fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_106-4"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-106">[106]</a></sup> Fish were sacred to the Syrian goddess <a href="/wiki/Atargatis" title="Atargatis">Atargatis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyde2008_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyde2008-107">[107]</a></sup> and, during her festivals, only her priests were permitted to eat them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyde2008_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hyde2008-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ichthus.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="445" data-file-width="902" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ichthus.svg/220px-Ichthus.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ichthus.svg/330px-Ichthus.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ichthus.svg/440px-Ichthus.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ichthus.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Ichthys" title="Ichthys">ichthus</a> is a Christian symbol of a fish signifying that the person who uses it is a Christian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyde2008_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hyde2008-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coffman2008_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coffman2008-108">[108]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jonah" title="Book of Jonah">Book of Jonah</a>, a work of Jewish literature probably written in the fourth century BC, the central figure, a <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a> named <a href="/wiki/Jonah" title="Jonah">Jonah</a>, is swallowed by a giant fish after being thrown overboard by the crew of the ship he is travelling on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherwood_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwood-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ziolkowski2007_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ziolkowski2007-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaines_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaines-111">[111]</a></sup> The fish later vomits Jonah out on shore after three days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherwood_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwood-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ziolkowski2007_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ziolkowski2007-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaines_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gaines-111">[111]</a></sup> This book was later included as part of the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>, or Christian <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> and a version of the story it contains is summarized in <a href="/wiki/Surah" title="Surah">Surah</a> 37:139-148 of the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christians</a> used the <i><a href="/wiki/Ichthys" title="Ichthys">ichthys</a></i>, a symbol of a fish, to represent Jesus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyde2008_107-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hyde2008-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coffman2008_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coffman2008-108">[108]</a></sup> because the Greek word for fish, ΙΧΘΥΣ Ichthys, could be used as an acronym for "Ίησοῦς Χριστός, Θεοῦ Υἱός, Σωτήρ" (Iesous Christos, Theou Huios, Soter), meaning "Jesus Christ, Son of God, Saviour".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyde2008_107-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hyde2008-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coffman2008_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-Coffman2008-108">[108]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">gospels</a> also refer to "fishers of men"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Feeding_the_multitude" title="Feeding the multitude">feeding the multitude</a>. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dharma_(Buddhism)" title="Dharma (Buddhism)">dhamma</a> of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, the fish symbolize happiness as they have complete freedom of movement in the water. Often drawn in the form of <a href="/wiki/Asian_carp" title="Asian carp">carp</a> which are regarded in the Orient as sacred on account of their elegant beauty, size and life-span.
</p><p>Among the <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deities</a> said to take the form of a fish are <a href="/wiki/Ika-Roa" title="Ika-Roa">Ika-Roa</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Polynesians" title="Polynesians">Polynesians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dagon#Fish-god_tradition" title="Dagon">Dagon</a> of various ancient <a href="/wiki/Semitic_people" title="Semitic people">Semitic peoples</a>, the shark-gods of <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaiʻi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matsya" title="Matsya">Matsya</a> of the Hindus. The <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrological</a> symbol <a href="/wiki/Pisces_(astrology)" title="Pisces (astrology)">Pisces</a> is based on a constellation of the <a href="/wiki/Pisces_(constellation)" title="Pisces (constellation)">same name</a>, but there is also a second fish constellation in the night sky, <a href="/wiki/Piscis_Austrinus" title="Piscis Austrinus">Piscis Austrinus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fish feature prominently in art and literature, in movies such as <i><a href="/wiki/Finding_Nemo" title="Finding Nemo">Finding Nemo</a></i> and books such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Old_Man_and_the_Sea" title="The Old Man and the Sea">The Old Man and the Sea</a></i>. Large fish, particularly sharks, have frequently been the subject of <a href="/wiki/Horror_film" title="Horror film">horror movies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thriller_(genre)" title="Thriller (genre)">thrillers</a>, most notably the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Jaws_(novel)" title="Jaws (novel)">Jaws</a></i>, which spawned a series of films of the <a href="/wiki/Jaws_(film)" title="Jaws (film)">same name</a> that in turn inspired similar films or parodies such as <i><a href="/wiki/Shark_Tale" title="Shark Tale">Shark Tale</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Snakehead_Terror" title="Snakehead Terror">Snakehead Terror</a></i>. Piranhas are shown in a similar light to sharks in films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Piranha_(1978_film)" title="Piranha (1978 film)">Piranha</a></i>; however, contrary to popular belief, the <a href="/wiki/Red-bellied_piranha" title="Red-bellied piranha">red-bellied piranha</a> is actually a generally timid scavenger species that is unlikely to harm humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Legends of half-human, half-fish <a href="/wiki/Mermaid" title="Mermaid">mermaids</a> have featured in folklore, including the stories of <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_Andersen" title="Hans Christian Andersen">Hans Christian Andersen</a>.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<h3><span id=".22Fish.22_or_.22fishes.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Fish"_or_"fishes"'>"Fish" or "fishes"</span></h3>
<p>Though often used interchangeably, in biology these words have different meanings. <i>Fish</i> is used as a singular noun, or as a plural to describe multiple individuals from a single species. <i>Fishes</i> is used to describe different species or species groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pauly2004_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pauly2004-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey19975_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey19975-120">[120]</a></sup> Thus a pond would be said to contain 120 fish if all were from a single species or 120 fishes if these included a mix of several species. The distinction is similar to that between people and peoples.
</p>
<h3><span id=".22True_fish.22_or_.22finfish.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"True_fish"_or_"finfish"'>"True fish" or "finfish"</span></h3>
<ul><li>In biology, the term <i>fish</i> is most strictly used to describe any animal with a <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">backbone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a> throughout life, and limbs (if any) in the shape of <a href="/wiki/Fin" title="Fin">fins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson20062_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson20062-23">[23]</a></sup> Many types of <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_animal" title="Aquatic animal">aquatic animals</a> with common names ending in "fish" are not fish in this <a href="/wiki/Fish#Diversity" title="Fish">sense</a>; examples include <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Starfish" title="Starfish">starfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crayfish" title="Crayfish">crayfish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>. In earlier times, even biologists did not make a distinction – sixteenth century natural historians classified also <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a>, whales, <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a>, even <a href="/wiki/Hippopotamus" title="Hippopotamus">hippopotamuses</a>, as well as a host of aquatic invertebrates, as fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-integrated_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-integrated-26">[26]</a></sup></li>
<li>In fisheries, the term <i>fish</i> is used as a collective term, and includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">mollusks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> and any <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_animal" title="Aquatic animal">aquatic animal</a> which is harvested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao_glossary_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-fao_glossary-121">[121]</a></sup></li>
<li>The strict biological definition of a fish, above, is sometimes called a <i>true fish</i>. True fish are also referred to as <i>finfish</i> or <i>fin fish</i> to distinguish them from other aquatic life harvested in fisheries or aquaculture.</li></ul>
<h3><span id=".22Shoal.22_or_.22school.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Shoal"_or_"school"'>"Shoal" or "school"</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">Shoaling and schooling</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:School_of_Pterocaesio_chrysozona_in_Papua_New_Guinea_1.jpg"><img alt="Photo of thousands of fish separated from each other by distances of 2 inches (51 mm) or less" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1224" data-file-width="1632" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/School_of_Pterocaesio_chrysozona_in_Papua_New_Guinea_1.jpg/220px-School_of_Pterocaesio_chrysozona_in_Papua_New_Guinea_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/School_of_Pterocaesio_chrysozona_in_Papua_New_Guinea_1.jpg/330px-School_of_Pterocaesio_chrysozona_in_Papua_New_Guinea_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/School_of_Pterocaesio_chrysozona_in_Papua_New_Guinea_1.jpg/440px-School_of_Pterocaesio_chrysozona_in_Papua_New_Guinea_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:School_of_Pterocaesio_chrysozona_in_Papua_New_Guinea_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>These <a href="/wiki/Goldband_fusilier" title="Goldband fusilier">goldband fusiliers</a> are <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">schooling</a> because their swimming is synchronised.</div></div></div>
<p>An assemblage of fish merely using some localised resource such as food or nesting sites is known simply as an <i>aggregation</i>. When fish come together in an interactive, social grouping, then they may be forming either a <i>shoal</i> or a <i>school</i> depending on the degree of organisation. A <i>shoal</i> is a loosely organised group where each fish swims and forages independently but is attracted to other members of the group and adjusts its behaviour, such as swimming speed, so that it remains close to the other members of the group. <i>Schools</i> of fish are much more tightly organised, synchronising their swimming so that all fish move at the same speed and in the same direction. Shoaling and schooling behaviour is believed to provide a variety of advantages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997375_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997375-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Examples:
</p>
<ul><li>Cichlids congregating at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lek_(mating_arena)" title="Lek (mating arena)">lekking</a> sites form an <i>aggregation</i>.</li>
<li>Many minnows and characins form <i>shoals</i>.</li>
<li>Anchovies, herrings and silversides are classic examples of <i>schooling</i> fish.</li></ul>
<p>The most common collective nouns for a group of fish in general are school and shoal. Both the words have evolved from the same common Dutch root 'schole' meaning a troop or crowd. While the words "school" and "shoal" have different meanings within biology, the distinctions are often ignored by non-specialists who treat the words as <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonyms</a>. Thus speakers of <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a> commonly use "shoal" to describe any grouping of fish, and speakers of <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a> commonly use "school" just as loosely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cephalochordate" title="Cephalochordate">Cephalochordate</a>
<ul><li><abbr aria-label="Extinct" style="border: none; text-decoration: none; cursor: inherit; font-weight: normal; font-style: normal;" title="Extinct">†</abbr><i><a href="/wiki/Pikaia" title="Pikaia">Pikaia</a></i></li>
<li><abbr aria-label="Extinct" style="border: none; text-decoration: none; cursor: inherit; font-weight: normal; font-style: normal;" title="Extinct">†</abbr><i><a href="/wiki/Cathaymyrus" title="Cathaymyrus">Cathaymyrus</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lancelet" title="Lancelet">Lancelet</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Olfactores" title="Olfactores">Olfactores</a>
<ul><li><abbr aria-label="Extinct" style="border: none; text-decoration: none; cursor: inherit; font-weight: normal; font-style: normal;" title="Extinct">†</abbr><i><a href="/wiki/Haikouella" title="Haikouella">Haikouella</a></i></li>
